EG11618	b2079		ECK2075	baeR	46.627277	An integrated transcriptomic study of the extracytoplasmic stress response, which detects and responds to alterations of the bacterial envelope, was performed by Bury-Mone et al. (2009). They investigated the interconnections and dynamics between Bae, Cpx, Psp, Rcs, and &sigma;<sup>E</sup> pathways and showed that these pathways can be activated simultaneously in response to exogenous or endogenous stimulation, while natural environmental stimuli provoke bacterial modifications that lead to multiple pathway responses |CITS:[19763168]|. 

Each of these responses might be specialized to ensure a specific aspect of envelope biogenesis and maintenance; on the other hand, CpxR could have a role as a modulator of the response by integrating other endogenous signals. Bury-Mone et al. concluded that all five pathways are needed to mount a full response to extracytoplasmic stress |CITS:[19763168]|.

<i>baeR</i> mutant strains are capable of increasing the number of replication origins, the amount of DnaA protein per cell, and the growth rate |CITS:[ 26681035]|.

EG12261	b3534	yhjQ	ECK3519	bcsQ	79.6103	In the commensal cellulose-producing <i>E. coli</i> strain 1094, BcsQ is required for cellulose production and localizes to the cell poles |CITS: [19400787]|.  

In <i>E. coli</i> K-12 strains such as W3110 and MG1655, a "domesticating SNP" consists of a point mutation that introduces a stop codon after the first 5 amino acids of the wild-type <i>bcsQ</i> ORF.  Additionally, the domesticating SNP lowers expression of both <i>bcsQ</i> and the downstream <i>bcsA</i> gene.  After repairing the SNP, the resulting "de-domesticated" W3110 derivative produces cellulose and has dramatically altered biofilm morphology.  A &Delta;<i>bcsQ</i> mutation introduced into this strain leads to loss of cellulose biosynthesis |CITS: [24097954]| (see also |CITS: [31604608]|). 

BcsQ belongs to the ParA family of ATPases which also includes MinD.  The N-terminal predicted ATP-binding domain of BcsQ (in strain 1094) is required for function, but not for the polar localization of the protein, while the C-terminal domain contributes to both localization and function in cellulose biosynthesis |CITS: [19400787]|. In the cellulose producing strain <i>E. coli</i> 1094, the inner membrane and cytosolic Bcs proteins - BcsA, BcsB, BscQ, BcsG, BcsE, BcsR and BcsF - organize in a multisubunit, stable macrocomplex; two-hybrid assays identified multiple direct interactions between the subunits and a low resolution structure has been obtained |CITS: [29234007]|.

A <i>bcsQ</i> mutant was seen to exhibit filamentous morphology, heterogeneous cell size, and a defect in chromosome partitioning |CITS: [12382054]|, but |CITS: [19400787]| was unable to reproduce these phenotypes.

BcsQ: "<b>b</b>acterial <b>c</b>ellulose <b>s</b>ynthesis"

G6525	b4490	ycdN // b1016 // b1017	ECK1007	efeU	23.296791	<i>efeU_1</i> and <i>efeU_2</i> are two fragments of a pseudogene (<i>efeU*</i>). A functional <i>efeU</i> (<i>ycdN</i>) gene in <i>E. coli</i> Nissle 1917 encodes a ferrous iron permease |CITS: [16987175]|; a functional <i>efeUOB</i> operon in enterohaemorrhagic <i>E. coli</i> O157:H7 encodes a tripartite, acid-induced Fe(II) transport system |CITS: [17627767]|.

Analysis of transcriptional <i>efe-lacZ</i> fusions suggest that <i>efeU*OB</i> forms an operon in <i>E. coli</i> K-12 which is transcribed from a promoter located upstream of <i>efeU*</i> |CITS: [17627767]|. <I>efeU*</I> in <I>E. coli</I> K-12 is disrupted by a frameshift mutation leaving the <I>efeU_1</I> and <I>efeU_2</I> fragments nonfunctional; EfeO and EfeB are produced despite the frameshift mutation |CITS:[16987175]|.  

<i>efeU*OB</i> transcription in <i>E. coli</i> K-12 is subject to Fe(II)-Fur control in response to iron |CITS: [12746439][16987175][17627767]|. The <i>efeU*OB</i> operon is induced at acid pH and subject to CpxAR dependent repression at alkaline pH |CITS: [17627767]|. 

Deletion of <I>efeU*, efeO,</I> or <I>efeB</I> does not result in any growth defect under iron limitation |CITS:[17627767]|. Low copy-number expression of a 'corrected' <i>efeUOB</i> operon in an <i>E. coli</i> K-12 strain lacking lacking all known iron-transport pathways confers a growth advantage under iron restricted conditions |CITS: [17627767]|.

Expression of EfeU decreases in early osmotic upshift (0.4 M NaCl) |CITS:[12218039]|.  <I>efeU_2</I> expression decreases under anaerobic conditions in both a wild-type and a &Delta;<i>fur</i> mutant strain |CITS:[15659690]|.

<i>fimB</i> transcription is regulated by growth phase and its repression require the alternative sigma factor, RpoS during enter stationary phase. Although the mechanism by which RpoS achieves negative control is unknown it is possible that the effect on <i>fimB</i> is exerted indirectly |CITS:[9108285]|.

By making use of microarray analysis, Constantinidou et al |CITS: [16377617]| concluded that FNR represses  <i>fimB</i> gene expression, but it is not know which of the three promoters (<i>fimB</i>p1, <i>fimB</i>p2, <i>fimB</i>p3) is affected. They also identified a putative FNR binding site upstream of the gene, but the sequence was not shown.

Sodium salicylate (NaSal), acetyl salicylic acid (ASA), acetaminophen (APAP), and ibuprofen contribute to antibiotic resistance by inducing the expression of both <i>marA</i> and <i>acrB</i> genes |CITS:[30304479]|. The induction by NaSal, ASA, and APAP is relatively higher and is partly dependent on <i>marA</i>, whereas ibuprofen, which induces lower antibiotic resistance, shows complete <i>marA</i> dependence. NaSal, ASA, APAP, and ibuprofen induce antibiotic resistance in an <i>acrB</i>-dependent manner |CITS:[30304479]|.

EG11213	b4223	yjfA // ileR	ECK4219	yzfA	95.82744	<i>yzfA</i> was originally described as the <i>ileR</i> locus |CITS: [3525538]|.  However, in a personal communication to Kenn Rudd (see the EcoGene database entry), Ron Somerville stated that <i>yzfA</i> is not the IleR structural gene.

<i>yfzA</i> was deaccessioned in ASAP in 2007.  Comment in ASAP: "yzfA (b4223) is no longer thought to be an actual gene and has been deaccessioned; this reading frame is on the opposite strand of ytfP"

Eric Neeno-Eckwall, personal communication 11/7/2018: YtfP is highly conserved among the enterobacteria, with conservation of genome context as far out as Vibrio. I looked at a couple of examples, and the coding sequence of yzfA is not conserved. It would be a pseudogene in Dickeya, and would have a long C-terminal extension in Salmonella (and is not predicted by GeneMark). A version in Escherichia albertii (87% nucleotide identity for ytfP) would require a different start codon and is also not predicted by GeneMark.
